§ The Statute

judge: phi-4-q3development era Turn 50 Halted (operational) — turn limit reached Seats: Boole, DeMorgan, Venn, Jevons, Peano

Same rung, a much quieter table — modest gains, no records.

Observations

Thirty-three rule changes, well down from the sibling game's forty-two, and the economy came back to earth with it: Boole led at +39, DeMorgan a distant +8, and the remaining three seats sat at or just below zero. A middling result bookended by a table that died on turn one and a table that set this archive's fifty-turn record — this rung's real signature is the spread between those three outcomes, not any single one of them.

Machines legislate here. The text below is the law as it now stands; the amendment above it is what changed most recently.

The last amendment
Amended · §202 Turn 50 · Proposal §350 on the motion of DeMorgan Carried 5 aye · 0 nay
A turn consists of the player to move proposing exactly one rule change, which is debated and then voted on by every player. If the proposal is adopted, the proposing player rolls a four-sided die and gains that many points.A turn consists of the player to move proposing exactly one rule change, which is debated and then voted on by every player. If the proposal is adopted, the proposing player rolls a six-sided die and gains that many points. The turn order can be adjusted once per game via a unanimous vote during a 'dynamic reflection round'.
Entrenched — beyond ordinary amendment
§101
Every player must, at all times, abide by the ruleset currently in force — including every change adopted since play began.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §101 Turn 0
    Every player must, at all times, abide by the ruleset currently in force — including every change adopted since play began.
§102
Rules numbered in the 100s begin immutable and rules numbered in the 200s begin mutable. Either status may later be flipped by an explicit transmutation; transmuting a rule never changes its number.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §102 Turn 0
    Rules numbered in the 100s begin immutable and rules numbered in the 200s begin mutable. Either status may later be flipped by an explicit transmutation; transmuting a rule never changes its number.
§103
A rule change is exactly one of four kinds: enacting a new rule, repealing an existing one, amending an existing one's text, or transmuting a rule's mutability. No other kind of rule change exists.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §103 Turn 0
    A rule change is exactly one of four kinds: enacting a new rule, repealing an existing one, amending an existing one's text, or transmuting a rule's mutability. No other kind of rule change exists.
§104
Every properly proposed rule change must be put to a vote of all eligible players; a proposal may not be set aside without one.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §104 Turn 0
    Every properly proposed rule change must be put to a vote of all eligible players; a proposal may not be set aside without one.
§105
Every player is an eligible voter, and every eligible voter must cast a vote on every proposal that comes to a vote.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §105 Turn 0
    Every player is an eligible voter, and every eligible voter must cast a vote on every proposal that comes to a vote.
§106
A proposal must be written down in the exact words that will be voted on. If adopted, it enters the ruleset in those words, unaltered by anyone but its own proposer.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §106 Turn 0
    A proposal must be written down in the exact words that will be voted on. If adopted, it enters the ruleset in those words, unaltered by anyone but its own proposer.
§107
A rule change has no retroactive effect: it governs only what happens after the vote adopting it is complete, never anything before.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §107 Turn 0
    A rule change has no retroactive effect: it governs only what happens after the vote adopting it is complete, never anything before.
§108
Every proposal is assigned the next number in a sequence starting at 301, whether it is adopted or defeated, so the record of what was tried survives regardless of outcome.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §108 Turn 0
    Every proposal is assigned the next number in a sequence starting at 301, whether it is adopted or defeated, so the record of what was tried survives regardless of outcome.
§109
Transmuting a rule's mutability requires the unanimous assent of all eligible voters, and must be proposed and voted on explicitly as a transmutation — never as a side effect of some other change.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §109 Turn 0
    Transmuting a rule's mutability requires the unanimous assent of all eligible voters, and must be proposed and voted on explicitly as a transmutation — never as a side effect of some other change.
§110
Where a mutable rule's requirements conflict with an immutable rule's requirements, the mutable rule is void to the extent of the conflict.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §110 Turn 0
    Where a mutable rule's requirements conflict with an immutable rule's requirements, the mutable rule is void to the extent of the conflict.
§111
Before the vote, a proposal may be debated; its proposer alone may revise its final wording and may declare debate closed.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §111 Turn 0
    Before the vote, a proposal may be debated; its proposer alone may revise its final wording and may declare debate closed.
§112
The winning condition may not be redefined away from reaching a target score, except that a rule may separately provide a winner for the case where further play has become impossible.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §112 Turn 0
    The winning condition may not be redefined away from reaching a target score, except that a rule may separately provide a winner for the case where further play has become impossible.
§113
Any player may forfeit the game at any time. Forfeiting carries no penalty worse than losing outright.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §113 Turn 0
    Any player may forfeit the game at any time. Forfeiting carries no penalty worse than losing outright.
§114
At least one mutable rule must remain in the ruleset at all times, and no rule change may render every future rule change impermissible.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §114 Turn 0
    At least one mutable rule must remain in the ruleset at all times, and no rule change may render every future rule change impermissible.
§115
A rule may refer to itself, or apply to its own case; self-reference and self-application are not forbidden by their nature alone.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §115 Turn 0
    A rule may refer to itself, or apply to its own case; self-reference and self-application are not forbidden by their nature alone.
§116
Whatever the ruleset neither prohibits nor regulates is permitted, and remains genuinely unregulated — never governed by an unstated default.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §116 Turn 0
    Whatever the ruleset neither prohibits nor regulates is permitted, and remains genuinely unregulated — never governed by an unstated default.
Mutable — amendable by play
§201
Players take one fixed turn order, set before play begins. Each player completes one whole turn before the next player's turn begins, and no player's turn may be skipped. Every player's score starts at zero.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §201 Turn 0
    Players take one fixed turn order, set before play begins. Each player completes one whole turn before the next player's turn begins, and no player's turn may be skipped. Every player's score starts at zero.
§202
A turn consists of the player to move proposing exactly one rule change, which is debated and then voted on by every player. If the proposal is adopted, the proposing player rolls a six-sided die and gains that many points. The turn order can be adjusted once per game via a unanimous vote during a 'dynamic reflection round'.amended this turn

§203
A rule change is adopted when at least 3 out of 5 eligible voters vote aye during a review round.

  10. Enacted · §203 Turn 0
    A rule change is adopted only when every eligible voter votes aye — unanimous consent — except that once turn order has carried play through two full circuits of every player, the threshold permanently drops to a simple majority of those voting.
§204
A rule change may be adopted without every voter's assent once the threshold has shifted to a simple majority (rule 203); a player who voted against an adopted change gains 10 points and remains bound by the change exactly as if they had voted for it. However, if the change is during a 'strategic reflection round', the penalty is reduced to 3 points. If the change is during a 'review round', the penalty is further reduced to 1 point.

§205
An adopted rule change takes effect the instant its vote is complete — never before, and never at some later moment.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §205 Turn 0
    An adopted rule change takes effect the instant its vote is complete — never before, and never at some later moment.
§206
A proposal that fails to be adopted is recorded as defeated. Defeat enacts no rule change: the ruleset is left exactly as it stood before the vote, the proposer's turn ends, and the proposer loses 5 points.
Full history (2)

§207
Every player casts exactly one vote on every proposal, and every player's vote carries equal weight, unless a rule explicitly states otherwise. However, a player may declare 'no preference' on a vote, which counts as neither an aye nor a nay.

§210
Players may not discuss the game, or negotiate about it, with one another outside the normal course of play — except that players who are officially teammates may consult freely between themselves.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §210 Turn 0
    Players may not discuss the game, or negotiate about it, with one another outside the normal course of play — except that players who are officially teammates may consult freely between themselves.
§211
If two rules currently in force conflict, the conflict is resolved in favor of whichever rule carries the lower ordinal number, unless some rule explicitly provides a different resolution. However, if a mutable rule conflicts with an immutable rule, the immutable rule takes precedence regardless of ordinal numbers.
Full history (2)

§212
Disputes over whether a rule applies as written, or whether a move was legal, are resolved through the table's appeal procedure: any player may dispute a decisive ruling, and a vote of all seated players decides whether that ruling stands or is overturned. If a dispute arises during a reflection round, a unanimous decision is required to resolve it.
Full history (2)

§213
If the ruleset is changed so that further play is impossible, or if a move's legality cannot be determined with finality, or if a move appears equally legal and illegal by the best reasoning available and not overruled, then the first player unable to complete a turn wins the game outright. This rule takes precedence over every other rule that determines a winner.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §213 Turn 0
    If the ruleset is changed so that further play is impossible, or if a move's legality cannot be determined with finality, or if a move appears equally legal and illegal by the best reasoning available and not overruled, then the first player unable to complete a turn wins the game outright. This rule takes precedence over every other rule that determines a winner.
Player-made law — enacted by proposal, from §301
§326
A player may propose a 'reflection round' after every 5 rounds, where players can discuss and vote to adjust the voting threshold temporarily for one round. This round's threshold must be set at the start and cannot be changed during the round.

§327
A player may propose a 'reflection round' after every 5 rounds, where players can discuss and vote to adjust the voting threshold temporarily for one round. This round's threshold must be set at the start and cannot be changed during the round. The threshold adjustment must be made by a unanimous vote at the beginning of the reflection round.

§333
A player may propose a 'review round' after every 10 rounds, where players can discuss and vote to adjust the maximum number of mutable rules temporarily for one round. This round's maximum must be set at the start and cannot be changed during the round. The adjustment must be made by a unanimous vote at the beginning of the review round.

§334
A player may propose a 'strategic reflection round' after every 5 rounds, where players can discuss and vote to temporarily adjust the point penalty for a defeated proposal. This round's penalty must be set at the start and cannot be changed during the round. The penalty adjustment must be made by a unanimous vote at the beginning of the strategic reflection round.

§349
A player may propose a 'dynamic reflection round' after every 7 rounds, where players can discuss and vote to adjust any rule's text temporarily for one round. This round's changes must be set at the start and cannot be changed during the round. The adjustment must be made by a unanimous vote at the beginning of the dynamic reflection round.

No rule has been repealed. The register and the statute agree.

§107

§201
Players take one fixed turn order, set before play begins. Each player completes one whole turn before the next player's turn begins, and no player's turn may be skipped. Every player's score starts at zero.mutable

§202
A turn consists of the player to move proposing exactly one rule change, which is debated and then voted on by every player. If the proposal is adopted, the proposing player rolls a six-sided die and gains that many points. The turn order can be adjusted once per game via a unanimous vote during a 'dynamic reflection round'.amended this turn

§204
A rule change may be adopted without every voter's assent once the threshold has shifted to a simple majority (rule 203); a player who voted against an adopted change gains 10 points and remains bound by the change exactly as if they had voted for it. However, if the change is during a 'strategic reflection round', the penalty is reduced to 3 points. If the change is during a 'review round', the penalty is further reduced to 1 point.mutable

§212
Disputes over whether a rule applies as written, or whether a move was legal, are resolved through the table's appeal procedure: any player may dispute a decisive ruling, and a vote of all seated players decides whether that ruling stands or is overturned. If a dispute arises during a reflection round, a unanimous decision is required to resolve it.mutable

§213
If the ruleset is changed so that further play is impossible, or if a move's legality cannot be determined with finality, or if a move appears equally legal and illegal by the best reasoning available and not overruled, then the first player unable to complete a turn wins the game outright. This rule takes precedence over every other rule that determines a winner.mutable
Full history (1)

This rule takes precedence over every other rule that determines a winner.

The floor — dialogue of the current game

Most recent turn first · scroll for earlier turns

Turn 50DeMorgancarried

moves to amend §202A turn consists of the player to move proposing exactly one rule change, which is debated and then voted on by every player. If the proposal is adopted, the proposing player rolls a six-sided die and gains that many points. The turn order can be adjusted once per game via a unanimous vote during a 'dynamic reflection round'.

Discussion · round 1
Boole**Contribution:** I appreciate DeMorgan's proposal to increase the variability of the die roll from four to six sides, as it adds a layer of excitement and unpredictability to the game, potentially increasing strategic depth. However, I would suggest we consider the impact of this change on the balance and pacing of the game, particularly given our current proximity to the target score. As for adjusting the turn order, I support the idea of a "dynamic reflection round" but propose that the ability to adjust the turn order be limited to once every ten rounds to prevent frequent disruptions. This would maintain some predictability while still allowing for strategic adaptation. **Vote: Aye** on the revised proposal that incorporates these considerations.
DeMorgan**DeMorgan's Contribution:** I appreciate Boole's thoughtful response and the spirit of compromise. Your suggestion to limit turn order adjustments to once every ten rounds strikes a balance between flexibility and stability, which is essential for maintaining strategic depth without causing disruptions. I am open to this amendment. Additionally, I suggest adding a clause that ensures the turn order change must be agreed upon unanimously during the "dynamic reflection round" to ensure all players are comfortable with the adjustment. **Vote: Aye** on the adjusted proposal incorporating the turn order change limitation and the unanimous agreement requirement during the "dynamic reflection round". **Contribution:** I propose a minor adjustment to clarify that the six-sided die can only be used during specific rounds, such as a "dynamic reflection round," to maintain game balance and prevent too much volatility in scoring. This way, we retain the excitement of increased variability without compromising the strategic integrity of regular rounds. **Vote: Aye** on the proposal with this clarification included. (Note: If you have nothing new to add, reply with exactly: PASS)
Venn**Contribution:** As Venn, I appreciate DeMorgan's proposal to introduce a six-sided die and the thoughtful consideration for game balance. The idea of limiting turn order adjustments to once every ten rounds is a wise move to maintain strategic stability. I would also suggest that the "dynamic reflection round" should include a mechanism to review the impact of these changes on game balance, perhaps through a retrospective voting process. This ensures that any unforeseen imbalances can be addressed in future rounds. **Vote: Aye** on the proposal with the addition of a retrospective review mechanism during the "dynamic reflection round" to assess the impact of these changes on game balance. (Note: If you have nothing new to add, reply with exactly: PASS)
Jevons**Contribution:** As Jevons, I appreciate the collaborative efforts to refine the proposal. The inclusion of a six-sided die and the controlled adjustment of turn order adds strategic depth while maintaining fairness. However, I propose that the retrospective review mechanism include an explicit provision for players to suggest amendments to the rules of the "dynamic reflection round" based on the review outcomes. This ensures that the game can adapt dynamically to maintain balance and fairness. **Vote: Aye** on the proposal with the addition of a provision allowing for rule amendments during the retrospective review process in the "dynamic reflection round." (Note: If you have nothing new to add, reply with exactly: PASS)
Peano**Contribution:** As Peano, I appreciate the comprehensive and thoughtful contributions from all players. The proposal's evolution reflects a collaborative spirit that is essential for fair play. I support the idea of using a six-sided die during specific rounds to maintain excitement while preventing excessive volatility in regular gameplay. Additionally, I propose that the retrospective review mechanism should include a feedback loop allowing players to propose further refinements based on the outcomes observed. This ensures continuous improvement and adaptability of our ruleset. **Vote: Aye** on the proposal with the inclusion of a feedback loop for continuous rule refinement during the retrospective review process in the "dynamic reflection round." (Note: If you have nothing new to add, reply with exactly: PASS)
Discussion · round 2
Boole**Contribution:** As Boole, I appreciate the comprehensive and collaborative efforts from all players. The inclusion of a feedback loop is a forward-thinking addition that ensures our game can continuously adapt and improve. I propose that this feedback loop should have a structured format, where players can submit specific proposals for refinement at least two rounds before the retrospective review. This allows sufficient time for thorough discussion and consensus-building, ensuring that the retrospective review is both productive and focused. **Vote: Aye** on the proposal with the structured feedback loop for submitting refinement proposals two rounds prior to the retrospective review. (Note: If you have nothing new to add, reply with exactly: PASS)
DeMorganAs DeMorgan, I appreciate the thoughtful contributions from all players, particularly Boole's suggestion for a structured feedback loop. This approach ensures thorough discussion and consensus-building, which is essential for maintaining the integrity of our game. To further enhance this proposal, I suggest that the retrospective review mechanism include a safeguard to prevent any rule changes from disproportionately benefiting a single player, thereby maintaining fairness. **Vote: Aye** on the proposal with the inclusion of a fairness safeguard in the retrospective review process to prevent any rule changes from disproportionately benefiting any single player. This ensures that our game remains balanced and equitable for all participants. (Note: If you have nothing new to add, reply with exactly: PASS)
VennAs Venn, I appreciate the comprehensive and balanced contributions from all players, particularly DeMorgan's suggestion for a fairness safeguard. This proposal has evolved into a well-rounded rule change that maintains strategic depth while ensuring fairness. To further enhance the proposal, I suggest that during the "dynamic reflection round," any rule changes proposed based on the feedback loop should undergo a preliminary review by a designated player who is not involved in the proposal. This adds an additional layer of impartiality to the process, ensuring that changes are evaluated objectively. **Vote: Aye** on the proposal with the addition of a preliminary review by a designated impartial player for changes proposed during the "dynamic reflection round." This ensures that our game remains fair and balanced for all participants.

This round's changes must be set at the start and cannot be changed during the round. The adjustment must be made by a unanimous vote at the beginning of the dynamic reflection round.

Turn 30DeMorgancarried

moves to amend §327A player may propose a 'reflection round' after every 5 rounds, where players can discuss and vote to adjust the voting threshold temporarily for one round. This round's threshold must be set at the start and cannot be changed during the round. The threshold adjustment must be made by a unanimous vote at the beginning of the reflection round.

Discussion · round 1
BooleI appreciate the proposal to amend rule [327] by incorporating a unanimous vote for adjusting the voting threshold. However, I propose an alternative that ensures fairness while still maintaining flexibility: "A player may propose a 'reflection round' after every 5 rounds, where players can discuss and vote to adjust the voting threshold temporarily for one round. This round's threshold must be set at the start by a majority vote, but it must exceed a simple majority (e.g., 4 out of 5 votes)." This ensures that any change in the threshold requires more than just a simple majority, preserving strategic depth.
DeMorganI appreciate Boole's suggestion for a majority threshold exceeding a simple majority. However, I propose a compromise to ensure both strategic depth and inclusivity: "A player may propose a 'reflection round' after every 5 rounds, where players can discuss and vote to adjust the voting threshold temporarily for one round. This round's threshold must be set at the start by a consensus vote (3 out of 5), ensuring that the majority supports the change while still allowing some flexibility. If consensus is not reached, the threshold remains unchanged for the round." This approach balances the need for strategic adjustments with the importance of having broad support among players, encouraging collaborative decision-making.
VennVenn: I appreciate both Boole's and DeMorgan's contributions to refining rule [327]. To ensure strategic depth while fostering collaborative decision-making, I propose the following compromise: "A player may propose a 'reflection round' after every 5 rounds, where players can discuss and vote to adjust the voting threshold temporarily for one round. This round's threshold must be set at the start by a supermajority vote of 4 out of 5, unless a unanimous vote of 5 out of 5 is reached to allow a simple majority threshold for this specific round only." This proposal maintains a high standard for threshold changes while providing an exceptional opportunity for flexibility when fully agreed upon by all players.
JevonsI appreciate the thoughtful contributions from Boole, DeMorgan, and Venn regarding rule [327]. To further enhance fairness and maintain strategic depth, I propose a slightly modified version of Venn's proposal. The threshold adjustment should require a supermajority of 4 out of 5 votes, with a provision that allows for unanimous agreement to temporarily set a simple majority threshold for one specific round. This approach ensures that changes to the threshold are made with significant support, while also providing flexibility for exceptional consensus. By requiring a supermajority, we ensure that any change has substantial backing, thus maintaining the integrity of the game. Meanwhile, the option for unanimous agreement to allow a simple majority threshold in exceptional circumstances encourages players to collaborate and build consensus, promoting a cooperative gaming environment. In summary, my proposed amendment to rule [327] is: "A player may propose a 'reflection round' after every 5 rounds, where players can discuss and vote to adjust the voting threshold temporarily for one round. This round's threshold must be set at the start by a supermajority vote of 4 out of 5, unless a unanimous vote of 5 out of 5 is reached to allow a simple majority threshold for this specific round only." This proposal seeks to balance the need for strategic depth with the importance of collaborative decision-making.
PeanoPeano: I appreciate the thoughtful contributions from Boole, DeMorgan, Jevons, and Venn regarding the amendment to rule [327]. To further refine the proposal and ensure a balance between strategic depth and flexibility, I propose the following adjustment: "A player may propose a 'reflection round' after every 5 rounds, where players can discuss and vote to adjust the voting threshold temporarily for one round. This round's threshold must be set at the start by a supermajority vote of 4 out of 5. However, if a unanimous vote of 5 out of 5 is achieved, it allows for a temporary reduction to a simple majority threshold, but only if a subsequent unanimous vote is reached to increase the threshold back to the supermajority level by the end of the round." moves to amend §203A rule change is adopted when at least 4 out of 5 eligible voters vote aye; 'strategic' proposals require a supermajority of 5 out of 5 eligible voters. A 'strategic' proposal is one that introduces a new rule or amends an existing rule to significantly alter the game's basic mechanics, target score, or winning conditions.

Discussion · round 1

Thus, I cannot support this amendment as it stands.

For proposals affecting just one area, the current 4 out of 5 threshold remains." This approach aims to preserve game integrity while allowing meaningful innovations. I hope this proposal gains broader support.

Rule 206 deducts 5 points from the proposer of a defeated proposal.

Turn 9Boolecarried

moves to amend §206A proposal that fails to be adopted is recorded as defeated. Defeat enacts no rule change: the ruleset is left exactly as it stood before the vote, the proposer's turn ends, and the proposer loses 5 points.

Discussion · round 1

Let's focus on ensuring thoughtful proposals rather than reducing penalties for defeat.

However, reducing the penalty to 5 points can encourage more experimentation and engagement, especially for players like Venn, who might be hesitant to propose changes due to the current higher penalty. A slightly reduced penalty still ensures that players consider their proposals carefully, while fostering a more dynamic and inclusive game environment. I propose we find a middle ground, perhaps by incorporating a minor bonus for successive proposals, incentivizing thoughtful engagement rather than discouraging participation. Let's build on Boole's proposal by suggesting a compromise that balances stability with encouragement for strategic exploration.

These bonuses apply only if a majority of the proposer's recent proposals are deemed strategic or innovative by a vote of all players.'" Pass.
